		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-98c1e61 e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="98c1e61" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container">
		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-900918f e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="900918f" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container">
		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-4c06191 e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="4c06191" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-6815762 elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="6815762" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
									<p><strong>Marks over scars: <em>Taare Zameen Par</em> as a modern social tragedy</strong></p>								</div>
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-4c787f1 elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="4c787f1" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
									<p><strong><span style="font-style: inherit;">Ankita Das</span></strong></p><p><span style="font-style: inherit; font-weight: inherit;">Department of English, </span>Dr. B.R. Ambedkar College, Nadia, West Bengal, India</p><p>Email: ankitadas741163@gmail.com</p>								</div>
				</div>
		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-f7a0f34 e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="f7a0f34" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-7e92bbe elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="7e92bbe" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
									<p><strong>Abstract:</strong></p><p>The film <em>Taare Zameen Par</em> (2007), directed by Aamir Khan, presents a powerful critique of the modern education system and the emotional suffering experienced by children who fail to conform to rigid academic expectations. This paper interprets the film as an example of modern social tragedy. Unlike classical tragedies that depict the downfall of kings or heroic figures, the tragedy in this narrative emerges from institutional pressure, parental aspiration, and the inability of society to recognize individual creativity. The protagonist Ishaan Awasthi, a child suffering from dyslexia, becomes the central figure through whom the film explores themes of alienation, misunderstanding, and psychological suffering. By applying the theoretical frameworks of Aristotelian tragedy, Raymond Williams’ concept of modern tragedy, and Arthur Miller’s theory of the tragic common man, the study demonstrates how the suffering of an ordinary child can represent a larger social crisis. The analysis also draws on Marxist criticism and Carl Rogers’ humanistic psychology to explain the institutional and emotional dimensions of the tragedy. Ultimately, Taare Zameen Par reveals how an education system obsessed with marks can suppress creativity and individuality, transforming childhood into a site of psychological struggle. The film therefore functions as both a social critique and a call for empathetic and inclusive education.</p><p>Keywords: Social tragedy, education system, dyslexia, modern tragedy, <em>Taare Zameen Par</em></p>								</div>
		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-fd2799f e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="fd2799f" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container" data-settings="{&quot;background_background&quot;:&quot;classic&quot;}">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-3529d4c elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="3529d4c" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
									<p><strong><em>How to cite this article:</em></strong></p><p>Das, A. (2026). Marks over scars: Taare Zameen Par as a modern social tragedy. Srijan: Global Review of Arts, Science &amp; Humanities, 1(1), 1–6.</p>								</div>

				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-0541ece elementor-widget elementor-widget-button" data-id="0541ece" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="button.default">
										<a class="elementor-button elementor-button-link elementor-size-sm" href="https://srijantirtha.in/elementor-page-991/" target="_blank">
						<span class="elementor-button-content-wrapper">
						<span class="elementor-button-icon">
				<svg aria-hidden="true" class="e-font-icon-svg e-fas-book-reader" viewBox="0 0 512 512" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg"><path d="M352 96c0-53.02-42.98-96-96-96s-96 42.98-96 96 42.98 96 96 96 96-42.98 96-96zM233.59 241.1c-59.33-36.32-155.43-46.3-203.79-49.05C13.55 191.13 0 203.51 0 219.14v222.8c0 14.33 11.59 26.28 26.49 27.05 43.66 2.29 131.99 10.68 193.04 41.43 9.37 4.72 20.48-1.71 20.48-11.87V252.56c-.01-4.67-2.32-8.95-6.42-11.46zm248.61-49.05c-48.35 2.74-144.46 12.73-203.78 49.05-4.1 2.51-6.41 6.96-6.41 11.63v245.79c0 10.19 11.14 16.63 20.54 11.9 61.04-30.72 149.32-39.11 192.97-41.4 14.9-.78 26.49-12.73 26.49-27.06V219.14c-.01-15.63-13.56-28.01-29.81-27.09z"></path></svg>			</span>

		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-bbdf6ba e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="bbdf6ba" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container">
		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-928db1f e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="928db1f" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container">
		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-b21997b e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="b21997b" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-5e69a07 elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="5e69a07" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
									<p><strong>Romanticism in Kylas Chunder Dutt’s <em>A Journal of Forty‑Eight Hours of the Year 1945</em> (1835)</strong></p>								</div>
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-ea22977 elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="ea22977" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
									<p>Ishani Nath</p><p><span style="font-style: inherit; font-weight: inherit;">Department of English, </span>Kanchrapara College, University of Kalyani, Nadia, West Bengal, India</p><p>Email: ishaninath457@gmail.com</p>								</div>
				</div>
		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-b126ad8 e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="b126ad8" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-dfca340 elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="dfca340" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
									<p><strong>Abstract:</strong></p><p>Kylas Chunder Dutt’s <em>A Journal of Forty‑Eight Hours of the Year 1945</em> is a remarkable early work of Indian English fiction that combines political imagination with literary experimentation. The narrative is generally interpreted as a pioneering nationalist text that anticipates later anti‑colonial writing in India. However, beyond its overt political themes, the text also reveals subtle Romantic elements that enrich its narrative texture. This paper explores the presence of Romanticism in Dutt’s narrative, focusing on the role of imagination, emotional intensity, and the aesthetic representation of nature. Through close textual reading, the study demonstrates how Romantic imagery and sentiment operate alongside themes of colonial resistance and national identity. The paper argues that the Romantic dimension of the narrative deepens the emotional resonance of the revolutionary storyline and provides an imaginative framework for articulating nationalist aspiration. By examining these Romantic elements, the research attempts to offer a new perspective on Dutt’s narrative and situates it within both Romantic literary tradition and the early history of Indian English literature.</p><p>Keywords:</p><p>Romanticism, nationalism, colonialism, Indian English literature, resistance, imagination, nature</p>								</div>
		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-47d3b64 e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="47d3b64" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container" data-settings="{&quot;background_background&quot;:&quot;classic&quot;}">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-faa530d elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="faa530d" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
									<p><strong><em>How to cite this article:</em></strong></p><p>Nath, I. (2026). Romanticism in Kylas Chunder Dutt’s A Journal of Forty-Eight Hours of the Year 1945 (1835). Srijan: Global Review of Arts, Science &amp; Humanities, 1(1), 7–13.</p>								</div>

		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-5b1e7b5 e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="5b1e7b5" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-bc300af elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="bc300af" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
									<p><strong>Beyond the Horizon: The Ocean as a Posthuman Agent in Disney’s Moana (2016)</strong></p>								</div>
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-b1f7ef5 elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="b1f7ef5" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
									<p>Nikita Mondal</p><p><span style="font-style: inherit; font-weight: inherit;">Department of English, </span>Sister Nivedita University</p><p>Email: mondalnikita42@gmail.com</p>								</div>
				</div>
		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-ec25e04 e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="ec25e04" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-b8e586f elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="b8e586f" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
									<p><strong>Abstract:</strong></p><p>Disney’s Moana (2016) presents the ocean not merely as a natural background but as a dynamic and responsive presence that participates actively in the narrative. The film portrays the ocean as a living and interactive entity that chooses Moana to restore the heart of Te Fiti and save the island of Motunui. This paper examines how the film reimagines the ocean as a posthuman agent, challenging traditional anthropocentric assumptions about the relationship between humans and nature. Drawing on posthuman ecocriticism and the theoretical insights of scholars such as Serpil Oppermann, Rosi Braidotti, and Stacy Alaimo, the study analyses the ocean’s actions as forms of agency that guide, protect, and communicate with human characters. Through close analysis of key scenes, the paper demonstrates how Moana visualizes the ocean as a co‑creator of narrative meaning rather than a passive environment. By portraying the ocean as an intelligent and ethically responsive presence, the film blurs the boundaries between human and anonhuman actors and proposes a relational ecological vision that challenges anthropocentric worldviews. The study ultimately argues that Moana contributes to contemporary discussions in posthumanism and environmental humanities by reimagining the ocean as an active participant in shaping human destiny and ecological balance.</p><p>Keywords: Posthumanism; Ocean; Anthropocene; Ecocriticism; Blue Humanities</p>								</div>
		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-cfb263f e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="cfb263f" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container" data-settings="{&quot;background_background&quot;:&quot;classic&quot;}">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-d2304dd elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="d2304dd" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
									<p><strong><em>How to cite this article:</em></strong></p><p>Mondal, N. (2026). Beyond the horizon: The ocean as a posthuman agent in Disney’s Moana (2016). Srijan: Global Review of Arts, Science &amp; Humanities, 1(1), 14–20.</p>								</div>

				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-411753d elementor-icon-list--layout-traditional elementor-list-item-link-full_width elementor-widget elementor-widget-icon-list" data-id="411753d" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="icon-list.default">
							<ul class="elementor-icon-list-items">
							<li class="elementor-icon-list-item">
											<span class="elementor-icon-list-icon">
							<svg aria-hidden="true" class="e-font-icon-svg e-fas-pen-fancy" viewBox="0 0 512 512" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg"><path d="M79.18 282.94a32.005 32.005 0 0 0-20.24 20.24L0 480l4.69 4.69 92.89-92.89c-.66-2.56-1.57-5.03-1.57-7.8 0-17.67 14.33-32 32-32s32 14.33 32 32-14.33 32-32 32c-2.77 0-5.24-.91-7.8-1.57l-92.89 92.89L32 512l176.82-58.94a31.983 31.983 0 0 0 20.24-20.24l33.07-84.07-98.88-98.88-84.07 33.07zM369.25 28.32L186.14 227.81l97.85 97.85 199.49-183.11C568.4 67.48 443.73-55.94 369.25 28.32z"></path></svg>						</span>
										<span class="elementor-icon-list-text">Marks over scars: Taare Zameen Par as a modern social tragedy by Ankita Das</span>
									</li>

		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-935ee33 e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="935ee33" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container">
		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-fbb56ce e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="fbb56ce" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container">
		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-a1a1997 e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="a1a1997" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-44db723 elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="44db723" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
									<p><strong>The Impact of Fever in English Literature: A Critical Study of Robinson Crusoe by Daniel Defoe and Things Fall Apart by Chinua Achebe</strong></p>								</div>
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-3350071 elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="3350071" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
									<p>Samima Aktar</p><p><span style="font-style: inherit; font-weight: inherit;">Department of English, Government General Degree College, Tehatta<br /><span style="font-size: 16px;">Email: aktarsamima955@gmail.com</span></span></p>								</div>
				</div>
		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-086e764 e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="086e764" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-c0bc060 elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="c0bc060" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
									<p><strong>Abstract:</strong></p><p>Fever frequently appears in literature not merely as a medical condition but as a symbolic device representing psychological crisis, spiritual transformation, and social instability. This paper examines the literary significance of fever in two influential novels: Daniel Defoe’s Robinson Crusoe (1719) and Chinua Achebe’s Things Fall Apart (1958). In Robinson Crusoe, fever functions as a decisive turning point that leads the protagonist toward repentance and religious awakening. In contrast, fever in Things Fall Apart operates within a communal cultural framework associated with Igbo cosmology and traditional spiritual beliefs. Through comparative textual analysis, the study demonstrates that fever functions both as a narrative catalyst and as a metaphor for wider historical tensions. By linking bodily suffering with moral, cultural, and spiritual transformation, fever reveals how literary narratives reflect the fragile relationship between the human body and the socio‑cultural world in which it exists.</p><p>Keywords: Fever, illness symbolism, colonialism, postcolonialism, repentance, transformation, literature and medicine</p>								</div>
		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-9e904e3 e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="9e904e3" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container" data-settings="{&quot;background_background&quot;:&quot;classic&quot;}">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-154a0eb elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="154a0eb" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
									<p><strong><em>How to cite this article:</em></strong></p><p>Aktar, S. (2026). The impact of fever in English literature: A critical study of Robinson Crusoe by Daniel Defoe and Things Fall Apart by Chinua Achebe. Srijan: Global Review of Arts, Science &amp; Humanities, 1(1), 21–27.</p>								</div>

		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-81cf688 e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="81cf688" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container">
		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-337ba2f e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="337ba2f" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container">
		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-eecd927 e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="eecd927" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-53c5629 elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="53c5629" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
									<p><strong>Sisterhood as a Survival System in a Patriarchal Society in Sense and Sensibility</strong></p>								</div>
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-5e64e1f elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="5e64e1f" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
									<p><strong>Indira Naru</strong></p><p><span style="font-style: inherit; font-weight: inherit;">Department of English, Rishi Bankim Chandra College, PG<br /><span style="font-size: 16px;">Email: innaru5656@gmail.com</span></span></p>								</div>
				</div>
		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-7b907b2 e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="7b907b2" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-ad68a4d elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="ad68a4d" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
									<p><strong>Abstract:</strong></p><p>Women’s lives in the late eighteenth and early nineteenth centuries were shaped by legal, social and economic systems that gave power and property to men. In such circumstances emotional security and economic survival often depended on marriage or the support of male relatives. Jane Austen’s Sense and Sensibility presents the relationship between Elinor and Marianne Dashwood as something deeper than a simple family bond. Their connection functions as a practical system of survival within a patriarchal society where women possess limited financial independence and little legal authority. This paper examines how the sisterly relationship between Elinor and Marianne helps them confront emotional vulnerability, social judgement and economic insecurity. Using feminist literary criticism and close textual reading, the analysis demonstrates that sisterhood in the novel operates through emotional care, moral guidance and shared economic adjustment. The study also compares this relationship with the bond between Jane and Elizabeth Bennet in Pride and Prejudice to show that Austen repeatedly presents female solidarity as a stabilizing force within patriarchal structures. The findings suggest that sisterhood should be understood not merely as affection but as a structured response to patriarchal limitations. Austen’s portrayal of female unity therefore becomes a subtle yet powerful commentary on women’s strategies for endurance and dignity within restrictive social systems.</p><p>Keywords: sisterhood, patriarchy, survival, Jane Austen, emotional support, economic vulnerability</p>								</div>
		<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-dd204ed e-con-full e-flex e-con e-child" data-id="dd204ed" data-element_type="container" data-e-type="container" data-settings="{&quot;background_background&quot;:&quot;classic&quot;}">
				<div class="elementor-element elementor-element-368887e elementor-widget elementor-widget-text-editor" data-id="368887e" data-element_type="widget" data-e-type="widget" data-widget_type="text-editor.default">
									<p><strong><em>How to cite this article:</em></strong></p><p>Naru, I. (2026). Sisterhood as a survival system in a patriarchal society in Sense and Sensibility. Srijan: Global Review of Arts, Science &amp; Humanities, 1(1), 28–35.</p>								</div>

				const lazyloadRunObserver = () => {
					const lazyloadBackgrounds = document.querySelectorAll( `.e-con.e-parent:not(.e-lazyloaded)` );
					const lazyloadBackgroundObserver = new IntersectionObserver( ( entries ) => {
						entries.forEach( ( entry ) => {
							if ( entry.isIntersecting ) {
								let lazyloadBackground = entry.target;
								if( lazyloadBackground ) {
									lazyloadBackground.classList.add( 'e-lazyloaded' );
								}
								lazyloadBackgroundObserver.unobserve( entry.target );
							}
						});
					}, { rootMargin: '200px 0px 200px 0px' } );
					lazyloadBackgrounds.forEach( ( lazyloadBackground ) => {
						lazyloadBackgroundObserver.observe( lazyloadBackground );
					} );
				};
				const events = [
					'DOMContentLoaded',
					'elementor/lazyload/observe',
				];
				events.forEach( ( event ) => {
					document.addEventListener( event, lazyloadRunObserver );
				} );
			</script>
